    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer1770192792474692441">Alphonse, a young Walloon officer, is travelling to join his regiment in Madrid in 1739. But he soon finds himself mysteriously detained at a highway inn in the strange and varied company of thieves, brigands, cabbalists, noblemen, coquettes and gypsies, whose stories he records over sixty-six days. The resulting manuscript is discovered some forty years later in a sealed </span>
  <span id="freeText1770192792474692441" style="display:none">Alphonse, a young Walloon officer, is travelling to join his regiment in Madrid in 1739. But he soon finds himself mysteriously detained at a highway inn in the strange and varied company of thieves, brigands, cabbalists, noblemen, coquettes and gypsies, whose stories he records over sixty-six days. The resulting manuscript is discovered some forty years later in a sealed casket, from which tales of characters transformed through disguise, magic and illusion, of honour and cowardice, of hauntings and seductions, leap forth to create a vibrant polyphony of human voices. Jan Potocki (1761-1812) used a range of literary styles - gothic, picaresque, adventure, pastoral, erotica - in his novel of stories-within-stories, which, like the Decameron and Tales from the Thousand and One Nights, provides entertainment on an epic scale.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1770192792474692441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_206266565" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206266565" />
    <a title="WILLIAM2" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4684009-william2"><img alt="WILLIAM2" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530030828p2/4684009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/206266565?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="WILLIAM2" class="user" itemprop="url" name="WILLIAM2" href="/user/show/4684009-william2">WILLIAM2</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=poland">poland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4684009-william2?shelf=20-ce">20-ce</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer206266565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1115041337291496487">Unlike many so called classic texts I have read this one doesn't seem to have dated much. At least not in its first half. The writing is thought by scholars to have begun about 1809. As <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Salman%20Rushdie' title='Salman Rushdie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Salman Rushdie</a> says in an attached blurb "...it reads like the most brilliant modern novel." I think that might be an effect of the recent English translation offered here that seems to give the text such a contemporary feel, like a modern-day historic novel.<br/><br/>The premise is that in the 1760s a Walloon officer name</span>
  <span id="freeText1115041337291496487" style="display:none">Unlike many so called classic texts I have read this one doesn't seem to have dated much. At least not in its first half. The writing is thought by scholars to have begun about 1809. As <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Salman%20Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie" rel="nofollow noopener">Salman Rushdie</a> says in an attached blurb "...it reads like the most brilliant modern novel." I think that might be an effect of the recent English translation offered here that seems to give the text such a contemporary feel, like a modern-day historic novel.<br /><br />The premise is that in the 1760s a Walloon officer named Alphonse (commissioned by Philip V) while traveling on leave in Andalucia, for centuries an Islamic land until the Reconquista, finds himself skirting a realm of ghosts, phantoms, specters, kindly bandits, storytelling gypsies and cabbalists. Because he does not at first succumb to the erotic offerings of these creatures--he has a very obnoxious sense of personal honor--he is able to preserve enough presence of mind to chronicle the many weird goings on. <br /><br />The book is full of the so called Magic Realism used by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com:443/search/search?q=Gabriel García Márquez" title="Gabriel García Márquez" rel="noopener">Gabriel García Márquez</a> and Rushdie himself. There are stories nested within stories nested within stories. The narrative is very straightforward. The characters wake up, go out, have dinner, come home, have sex, go to sleep, get up in the morning, and so on, and all of this action occurs during the briefest passages of text. There is the sense of the action moving full-tilt, almost out of control, but never really. It is only the impression created by the author's highly compressed style.<br /><br />Among the treats offered by the narrative are vast underground hideouts carved out of the stone, sun-scorched landscapes à la <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Don%20Quixote" title="Don Quixote" rel="nofollow noopener">Don Quixote</a>, convincing erotic encounters between men and women, abrupt murders, sometimes by the score. At a haunted inn phantoms show up at the stroke of midnight, though it is not known from whence the tolling comes. A motif of two men hanged on a gibbet, supposedly brothers of the bandit Zoto, who tells his story here, recurs throughout the early pages. At night the men leave the gibbet and get into mischief.<br /><br />There are strange elixirs to be drunk, seeming transportations through time and space, usually during a dream. On the whole the book is a kind of onieric wonderland where men are men and women are women of a thankfully extinct old school, except when they're murdering succubi who only wish to eat young men because of the wonderful effect their blood has on the demonic constitution. <br /><br />Then the Walloon officer succumbs, as he must, to the charms of the two Muslim women, who from the start have told him they are his cousins. A man who watches their erotic encounter sees only Alphonse sexually intimate with the two hanged men. From then on Alphonse seems to take some leave of his senses and is never sure if those Muslim women are his cousins / defacto wives or not. He sees them here in a pair of gypsy sisters, there in two women walking in the desert, but again it's not them. Later, he casts caution to the wind when he goes to meet them in an underground <i>chambre d'amour</i>. Who can blame him? It's either go insane or enjoy great if perhaps demonic sex with hot sisters!<br /><br />In the meantime the gypsy leader tells his story, the geometer or mathematician tells his, the Wandering Jew tells his, the two Muslim "cousins" tell theirs, the male cabbalist tells his, the female cabbalist tells hers, and so on. All of the characters seek to tell stories that seem realistically within their realm of competence/experience. It is only the geometer's tale that seems to falter in the mid to late stages. One gets the impression that author Potocki had committed himself to a line of disquisition that he could not sustain. An astonishing novel of enormous complexity that is nevertheless highly readable, even difficult to put aside when sleep calls. Please read it.<br /><br />PS. Some time later I began reading Matthew G. Lewis's Gothic horror novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=The%20Monk" title="The Monk" rel="nofollow noopener">The Monk</a>. It seems a likely model for Potocki.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1115041337291496487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6037783924519086093">I think I read this back in student days, I only think and with out certainty not for the usual reasons, but on account of the extreme unlikeness of what I recall, a Gothic Arabian nights with a framing narrative of the discovery of the eponymous manuscript by a traveller in Spain (a soldier from Belgium), which unleashes a continual plunging into stories, bizarre and convoluted, written by a Pole in French <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ this is probably the most reasonable part of the entire spectacle <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide s</a></span></span>
  <span id="freeText6037783924519086093" style="display:none">I think I read this back in student days, I only think and with out certainty not for the usual reasons, but on account of the extreme unlikeness of what I recall, a Gothic Arabian nights with a framing narrative of the discovery of the eponymous manuscript by a traveller in Spain (a soldier from Belgium), which unleashes a continual plunging into stories, bizarre and convoluted, written by a Pole in French <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ this is probably the most reasonable part of the entire spectacle <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. It all sounds a little too unlikely to be true, and yet apparently it exists. If life isn't a dream then perhaps is is the unfurling of nested narratives, each knock on the door heralding not escape but immersion into further stories.<br /><br />Indeed the more I think about it, the less possible it seems that such a devilish book would actually exist, perhaps I just found some old thing in the back rooms of the library and then dreamt the whole thing?</span>

<div id="review_2656694754" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2656694754" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2656694754?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781101491546" href="/book/show/9985619-the-manuscript-found-in-saragossa">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=penguin-classics">penguin-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=pre-1900s">pre-1900s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=voluminous">voluminous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=picaresque">picaresque</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=eastern-europe">eastern-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2656694754" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13626580348508894766">And swaggering in at a lithe 630 pages, middleweight champion of Eastern Europe, known as the Polish <i>Decameron</i>, blast them vuvuzelas for <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i>, a Spanish picaresque novel written in French by a polymathic aristocrat and suicide. Across sixty-six nights, Walloon officer Alphonse resists the erotic lure of Islamic conversion in the form of two Islamic temptresses, and listens to a series of yarns-within-yarns-within-yarns, from such eccentrics as the geometer Velásquez,</span>
  <span id="freeText13626580348508894766" style="display:none">And swaggering in at a lithe 630 pages, middleweight champion of Eastern Europe, known as the Polish <i>Decameron</i>, blast them vuvuzelas for <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i>, a Spanish picaresque novel written in French by a polymathic aristocrat and suicide. Across sixty-six nights, Walloon officer Alphonse resists the erotic lure of Islamic conversion in the form of two Islamic temptresses, and listens to a series of yarns-within-yarns-within-yarns, from such eccentrics as the geometer Velásquez, with his algebraic equations on love and religion, the Gypsy Chief, with his long misadventures involving a meddlesome squire Busqueros (an inverse Sancho Panza), and the Wandering Jew, a rather boring man prone to retelling old biblical tales. The novel is an epic monster, a monument to classic and timeless storytelling that happily shares a plinth with <i>The Arabian Nights</i> for sheer logorrheic magnificence. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13626580348508894766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Beata" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47571190-beata"><img alt="Beata" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505761623p2/47571190.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beata" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beata" href="/user/show/47571190-beata">Beata</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_121157504" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/121157504" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/121157504?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer121157504" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4586238479632541737">Potocki brought a little bit of everything to this book of tales within tales within tales: gothic horror, <i>bildungsroman</i>, swashbuckling adventure, <i>picaresque</i> reminiscent of the great <i>Lazarillo de Tormes</i>, philosophical and theological exposition, libertine erotica, political intrigue, travelogue—in other words, a true <i>olla podrida</i> of styles, narrated in an arch, dry, and ultra-witty voice that has been admirably delivered from the French original by the English scholar Ian Maclean. <i>The Manuscript</i></span>
  <span id="freeText4586238479632541737" style="display:none">Potocki brought a little bit of everything to this book of tales within tales within tales: gothic horror, <i>bildungsroman</i>, swashbuckling adventure, <i>picaresque</i> reminiscent of the great <i>Lazarillo de Tormes</i>, philosophical and theological exposition, libertine erotica, political intrigue, travelogue—in other words, a true <i>olla podrida</i> of styles, narrated in an arch, dry, and ultra-witty voice that has been admirably delivered from the French original by the English scholar Ian Maclean. <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i>, discovered by a French officer during the Napoleonic seizure of the eponymous city, is ostensibly the diary recorded—over the course of sixty-six story-filled days—by Alphonse van Worden, a young Walloon officer on his way to Madrid to join an elite regiment of the Spanish monarch. Honour-bound to make his way through the wild, rugged, and ominous Sierra Morena—a range home to fearsome bandits, gypsies, Moorish refugees, and, portentously, evil spirits and demons in service to the Archfiend—the straight-laced Alphonse, deserted by his frightened servants, determinedly lodges himself in an abandoned, haunted hostelry close to a ghastly public gallows—<i>Los Hermanos</i>—from which dangle the hideously disfigured bodies of two local sibling bandits. From this fateful decision the young <i>caballero</i> will find himself spending the next sixty-six days being regaled, tested, tempted, and discomfited by a parade of characters and entities that he encounters as he journeys through the shadowy vales and gnarled peaks of northern Andalusia; ranging from a pair of lascivious muslim sisters/succubi, to a one-eyed, emaciated automaton whose mind has been claimed by the mountain's madnesses, to the Geometer Velásquez, whose quiet and decent autodidact father—a man robbed of his dukedom and his soulmate by his eloquent and cunning Frenchified brother—provides what is perhaps the most moving and resplendent of the novel's many monologues.<br /><br />This is an immensely entertaining and thoroughly readable book. It becomes apparent that Potocki must have originally planned <i>TMFIS</i> to lean more towards the gothic horror stylings of works such as <i>The Monk</i>, only to subsequently steer his literary vessel away from such benighted waters and into the brighter streams of enlightenment naturalism, philosophical speculation and roguish adventure. Although this does not detract from the superb quality of Potocki's writing, it <i>does</i>, in my opinion, cause the story to lose some of its wonderful sense of mystery and eeriness. This transformation occurs around the time of the appearance of the verbose Gypsy chieftain—a central figure to the stories, one who functions as a hub around which the other tales encircle and entwine. The accumulation of stories range from the Old World to the New, from the ancient realm which witnessed the birth of Abraham through to the first half of the eighteenth century, though a majority of the action takes place in a beautifully and majestically rendered Spain in the waning days of the Habsburg dynasty. The way that characters and plot-lines in the various tales interact with and encounter each other—guided by the mischievous hand of coincidence and the stentorian hand of fate—proves eminently enjoyable for the reader; and the breathless declarations of love, the amorous encounters, the dashing swordplay, cunning intrigues, faithless abandonments, and devilish temptations—often pitting stoic and taciturn Spaniards against their more emotional European brethren—rush the reader headlong through the sixty-six days of historic, apocryphal, and cryptic reminiscences.<br /><br />In the introduction, Maclean acknowledges that several critics have complained about the ending that Potocki fashioned, about the sense of letdown in the author's method of tying up all of the various story lines and loose threads. Indeed, Potocki had written different, and differing, drafts of several of the daily chapters, and it is still debated whether the current edition represents the <i>definitive</i> assemblage of the Polish polymath's imaginative fiction. However, such complaints overlook the sheer <i>readability</i> of <i>The Manuscript</i>. Surrounded as I am by bookshelves, every wall bearing tomes that haunt me with the knowledge that, were I to live <i>two</i> lives, I might not make it through all of them, I often found myself tempted to abandon this collection of tall-tales to move on to more meaty fare; and yet, after telling myself I would partake of just <i>one</i> more story, I would inevitably get drawn in, held rapt while the hours whistled by and another week in textual time had passed—hours in which not the slightest trace of boredom could insert itself into page after page of crackling, razor-honed wit. That, to me, is the ultimate testament to an author's greatness: when he has drawn you once within his literary bear hug, you cannot resist the continual desire to go back for another until that melancholy moment arrives when there are no more embraces left for him to give.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4586238479632541737" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1762543005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1762543005" />
    <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes"><img alt="Nancy Oakes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507555979p2/900340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1762543005?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nancy Oakes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nancy Oakes" href="/user/show/900340-nancy-oakes">Nancy Oakes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781101491546" href="/book/show/9985619-the-manuscript-found-in-saragossa">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=translated-fiction">translated-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/900340-nancy-oakes?shelf=fiction-from-poland">fiction-from-poland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1762543005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6365892968446463821">First things first: do NOT read anything that gives away spoilers about this book because, in my opinion, it will completely wreck the reading experience. This book channels down to an ending that should not be revealed at all, and you really will do yourself a disservice by knowing it ahead of time. <br/><br/>Believe it or not, the moment I turned the last page I wanted to read this book again. Given its 600-plus pages, that says a lot, and I ended up not rereading it, but I very easily could have. I lov</span>
  <span id="freeText6365892968446463821" style="display:none">First things first: do NOT read anything that gives away spoilers about this book because, in my opinion, it will completely wreck the reading experience. This book channels down to an ending that should not be revealed at all, and you really will do yourself a disservice by knowing it ahead of time. <br /><br />Believe it or not, the moment I turned the last page I wanted to read this book again. Given its 600-plus pages, that says a lot, and I ended up not rereading it, but I very easily could have. I loved this book and I loved the people in it, but I spent most of the time in awe of the author's imagination.<br /><br />I will say right up front that this book will not be for everyone. It can be incredibly challenging because of the way it is written as a set of stories within stories within stories, which are often stopped and picked up again later rather than just finished at once, which in a couple of cases may require some backtracking. Reader expectations also play a role here. For example, I was reading Amazon reviews and came across one from a very disappointed reader who said that he was upset because he'd started this book with the expectation of a "fantasy work" but instead ended up with literary fiction. No comment on that one, but my point is that it's best to just go into it without any preconceived notions, because really, there's so much going on between these covers and so many different literary styles used here that to give it any sort of label would just flat out be folly. As the back cover blurb says, it's "entertainment on an epic scale," and really, that's how I'd approach it. In short, relax and go with the flow and you will be rewarded.<br /><br />The nature of this novel is such that I can't give out much detail, but the back-cover blurb also reveals that these tales consist partly of "characters transformed through disguise, magic and illusion," and that idea, more than any other, plays out over and over again throughout this book. One such story made me laugh out loud, but there are spots of humor everywhere. And there's so much more, including arcane and esoteric lore, demons, ghosts, political intrigue, Satan himself, and the Holy Inquisition, and there is not a dull moment to be had in this book. <br /><br />I loved it -- others may not share my experience, but it's one of those rare books that left me with a sense of loss after finishing it, knowing I'd come to the end. Each and every second with this book was just pure reading bliss. <br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.oddlyweirdfiction.com/2018/02/the-manuscript-found-in-saragossa-by.html">http://www.oddlyweirdfiction.com/2018...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6365892968446463821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1456183700" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1456183700" />
    <a title="[P]" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40516522-p"><img alt="[P]" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1486240560p2/40516522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1456183700?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="[P]" class="user" itemprop="url" name="[P]" href="/user/show/40516522-p">[P]</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781101491546" href="/book/show/9985619-the-manuscript-found-in-saragossa">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1456183700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14926527615680738825">I tend to introduce these reviews with a story or anecdote inspired by the text in question, something, in most cases, from my own past or present life. So as I came to write about Jan Potocki’s The Manuscript Found in Saragossa I was understandably perturbed when I realised that group sex [specifically threesomes] is so central to the novel’s plot. As much as I want to engage and entertain the reader, to build a relationship with the reader, I don’t much fancy going <i>there</i>. Even a self-obsessed </span>
  <span id="freeText14926527615680738825" style="display:none">I tend to introduce these reviews with a story or anecdote inspired by the text in question, something, in most cases, from my own past or present life. So as I came to write about Jan Potocki’s The Manuscript Found in Saragossa I was understandably perturbed when I realised that group sex [specifically threesomes] is so central to the novel’s plot. As much as I want to engage and entertain the reader, to build a relationship with the reader, I don’t much fancy going <i>there</i>. Even a self-obsessed blabbermouth has his limits.<br><br>In which case, what else should I focus on? Well, The Manuscript could be said to be a Gothic novel, with ghosts [and Satan!] featuring heavily, and I did once, as a child, apparently claim to have seen one sitting on the end of my bed, but that was likely the overactive imagination of a troubled little boy. I could, instead, write something about Jan Potocki himself, and how it is said that that he killed himself with a silver bullet, fashioned from the handle of a sugar bowl, which is certainly a suitably macabre anecdote. But, in the end, I have come to see that none of that is necessary, because what is most telling, most relevant, relative to this novel, is precisely my desire to share stories, my love of inventing, dramatising and embellishing, my need, you might say, to rummage around in my memories and work the details of my life into short narratives.<br><br>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa begins with a brief passage about how the book was, well, found in Saragossa by an unnamed French military man, who is later captured by the Spanish. Once under arrest he requests that he be able to keep the manuscript, which, as it is written in Spanish, he can only fully understand when it is translated and read to him by a Spanish captain. Therefore, before even entering the main body of the work, one has got a taste of how tricksy and shifting and tangled, how difficult to pin down, the book is: it is, to reiterate, the story of a manuscript written in Spanish…discovered by a Frenchman…translated out loud by a Spaniard…then written down in French. And yet it was actually authored by a Polish Count […although this too is subject to debate].<br><br>The following 600 pages are then given over to a mind-bending number of stories, stories within stories, and stories within stories within stories, etc., that take place mostly in Spain, France and Italy. There is, however, also a strong framing narrative, involving a young Wolloon Guard, Alphonse Von Worden, and his peregrinations through the possibly haunted Sierra Moreno and beyond, in the company of, amongst others, cabbalists, sexy lesbian Muslim sisters [who may be succubi], gypsies, bandits, and hanged men. For me, it is this that sets The Manuscript Found in Saragossa apart from other well-known books of this sort. The Arabian Nights and The Decameron, for example, are wonderful, but the framing narrative in each is just that: it is a thin [i.e. underdeveloped], less-than-engaging device that merely serves to tie the more entertaining tales together. Yet with Potocki’s work the frame is probably the most enjoyable [ or certainly the most intriguing] aspect of the novel, and I was always eager to get back to it, even though the other stories, with the exception of The Wandering Jew’s, were also able to effortlessly hold my attention.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449411488i/17282541._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>[One of Zoto’s brothers, from the film version of the novel]<br><br>One would expect with this kind of novel that there wouldn’t be a great deal of character depth or development, but that isn’t necessarily the case here. I certainly wouldn’t call any of the main characters complex, but Potocki does provide backstories, and explanations or justifications as to their personalities or behaviour. For example, in one of the stories we are told how Alphonse’s father was an expert on duelling, and duelling etiquette, and how he impressed upon his son the importance of honour and fearlessness; indeed, he once wanted the young boy thrashed when he admitted that he would be frightened if ever in the presence of ghosts. Therefore, one understands, in retrospect, why Alphonse refused to turn back even when warned twice about travelling through the Sierra Moreno, and why he appears to take all the strange goings-on in his stride. Furthermore, throughout the framing narrative Alphonse’s honour is put to the test. After giving the two Muslim sisters his word that he would not think ill of them, no matter what he was told or experienced, he is frequently asked to denounce them, but steadfastly refuses, and is, in fact, generally suspicious of anyone who wants him to doubt them.<br><br>I briefly mentioned Alphonse’s father in the preceding paragraph, and it is worth noting that the relationship between parents and children, specifically fathers and their children, plays a key role in most of the stories. Potocki’s fathers tend to be demanding of their offspring and/or subject to some peculiar preoccupation themselves. Take Valasquez, the geometrician, whose father insists that he avoid geometry and mathematics, and learn how to dance instead; or the cabbalist Rebecca, whose father, also a cabbalist, devotes his life to the art, and later insists that his daughter marry two demi-Gods. What the author shows in this instance, and in many other stories, is how one’s parents influence the direction of one’s life and help to mould the person that you become. Rebecca feels pressurised into pursuing cabbala, which does not interest her as much as her father and brother, and considers it an impediment to her living her life as she would like, taking a mortal husband and having children of her own.<br><br>Eventually Rebecca gives up cabbala, and one sees in this another of the novel’s motifs, which is that of things or people changing in some way or becoming something else. The most obvious, and repeated, example of this is the two hanged men, who we are initially informed are Zoto’s brothers [and therefore bandits], but who are later revealed to be shepherds, executed by the authorities in place of the brothers. Throughout, many of the characters have some experience of the two men, which invariably involves them coming down from the gallows and taking another form – such as the two Muslim sisters, Emina and Zubaida – and attempting to, or succeeding in, seducing them. Moreover, there is some debate as to whether the men are ghosts or vampires, or even whether they are, in fact, supernatural at all.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449411488i/17282542._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>[Emina and Zubaida, and Alphonse]<br><br>As a reviewer you want to identify, and discuss, the author’s aims, his ideas; you want to be able to say what the point is of all that you have read. But one of the features of The Manuscript is that it doesn’t appear to have any overriding, unifying theme[s]. Take the stuff about change, you might say that it is intended to highlight how things are not always what they seem, to warn you that you should not judge too rashly; or perhaps you could see it all as a comment on how life is full of twists and turns, how it is rarely ever stable and consistent. Yet I don’t really buy any of that, which is to say that, yes, life is not always consistent, but I don’t think the author was too concerned with communicating that idea to his audience. I think, as hinted at in my introduction, that the book is simply a very fine example of [a love of] the art of story telling; it is the product of someone revelling in it and having fun, rather than that of a man wanting to instruct or teach or philosophise. And sometimes that is just what you need: mindless fun, that doesn’t overtax your brain or play on your emotions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14926527615680738825" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11721462001320953515" style="display:none">This may be the best Polish novel ever written. Potocki was a member of one of the leading noble families of Poland. During the Napoleonic Wars he served as an officer with the French army. He appears to have taken a series of soldier's campfire tales and strung them together to create a work very similar to Antoine Galland's translation of the Tales of the Arabian nights. To this Potocki adds Masonic and Kabbalistic elements. It is a brio performance by any measure. Try to find Jerzy Haas's brilliant movie adaption once you are done reading this remarkable book. Lu en francais.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10855256335434416835">What a book! <br/>It is a classic piece of literature from the 19th century; is is a voyage into literatures most consecrated vaults. Gothic, Erotic, Picaresque, and Pastoral genres inundated this complex and vividly striking tale. It is a brilliant mix of Don Quixote, The Decameron, and The Arabian Nights into a unique book that deserves its own status as a literary giant. <br/>The best way to describe it? "It is a riddle wrapped in a mystery inside an enigma".<br/>I already miss it....</span>

<div id="review_97164570" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/97164570" />
    <a title="Drew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1200912-drew"><img alt="Drew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566807838p2/1200912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/97164570?book_show_action=true">Apr 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drew" href="/user/show/1200912-drew">Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer97164570" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10166479920336849290">I've never been a particularly fast reader and this book was consumed in the smallest of sips until I was stuck in hospital for a week and swallowed the last 300 pages in one gulp. <br/>Jan Potocki sounds like a character that Jan Potocki would invent: nobleman, warrior, diplomat, poet, lover, etc., etc. According to legend he shot himself with a decorative strawberry from the top of his silver sugar canister. Each day he filed away at it for a few strokes and when it was through he settled his bills</span>
  <span id="freeText10166479920336849290" style="display:none">I've never been a particularly fast reader and this book was consumed in the smallest of sips until I was stuck in hospital for a week and swallowed the last 300 pages in one gulp. <br />Jan Potocki sounds like a character that Jan Potocki would invent: nobleman, warrior, diplomat, poet, lover, etc., etc. According to legend he shot himself with a decorative strawberry from the top of his silver sugar canister. Each day he filed away at it for a few strokes and when it was through he settled his bills with a silver berry. <br />Of course, this story isn't part of the novel, but it might as well be. The whole story cycle is wrapped in various layers of narrative: a anonymous French officer who discovers the eponymous manuscript; Alfons von Worden, the young Flemish nobleman on whom the story centres; and the various other protagonists whose stories we are party to. <br />Initially the book delivers well on creepiness and ghoulish goings-on, but as it advances it becomes increasingly earthbound, or rather earth-scouring and delving, because we are dragged from the caves and mountains of Andalusia to Madrid, the New World, North Africa, Sicily and Spain, the Hapsburg empire, Egypt, Palestine and beyond. <br />All the while we hear are stories of the "I was born the first son of a grandee in the largest town in Murcia" kind. They are on some level formulaic, but also charming with a lightness (or complete absence) of characterisation. Here action is all. <br />I don't know if the above qualify as reservations, but I would certainly not recommend this book to everyone. It packs a massive amount of stories into a relatively small space, has a density that might not be everyone's cup of tea, and follows a relentless course through metaphysics, cabala, Islam, picaresque, Gothic horror and adventure novel. It's also a fascinating meditation on romanticism and anti-modernity that only reveals its true colours very late in the game. <br />If that doesn't put you off, by all means give it a go. You could be pleasantly surprised. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10166479920336849290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_42329786" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42329786" />
    <a title="Steve" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1882205-steve"><img alt="Steve" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537106707p2/1882205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42329786?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steve" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steve" href="/user/show/1882205-steve">Steve</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42329786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15367641956072911178">There's a fine film, <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Saragossa_Manuscript_%28film%29' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Saragossa Manuscripts</a>, championed by Jerry Garcia of the Grateful Dead, that captivated me when I saw it in 1968. But the film is a smidgeon of the book, a what awaits readers . <br/><br/>I concluded after two readings of this book that it is humanly impossible to grasp it on a single reading. Like many other great writers, Potocki wrote to be reread. His reader must be sufficiently entranced after a first reading want to find out what lies at its heart - indeed, to see if it even</span>
  <span id="freeText15367641956072911178" style="display:none">There's a fine film, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Saragossa_Manuscript_%28film%29" rel="nofollow noopener">The Saragossa Manuscripts</a>, championed by Jerry Garcia of the Grateful Dead, that captivated me when I saw it in 1968. But the film is a smidgeon of the book, a what awaits readers . <br /><br />I concluded after two readings of this book that it is humanly impossible to grasp it on a single reading. Like many other great writers, Potocki wrote to be reread. His reader must be sufficiently entranced after a first reading want to find out what lies at its heart - indeed, to see if it even <i>has</i> a heart, or a main idea. The book is that quirky, that convoluted, that hard to take in as a whole. So it was in this exploratory spirit that I began my second reading. Today I can say this: when reread with care - my copy's every page is dotted with notes and links from one page to the next - and when reread with a view to getting Potocki's take on ALL of the societies his book depicts - I gained a rich understanding, nation by nation, of the entire Mediterranean world of the late 1600's. This includes the complex interactions of diverse strains of Jewish, Christian and Muslim faiths. (Potocki, as a Polish diplomat, was intimately famkiliar with most all the countries depicted in his book. The man even visited Mongolia! And his disquistions, in the person of the eccentric "geometer" Velasquez, on philosophy ("Don Leibnitz"), science ("Don Newton") and religion in the 37th and 39th chapters, are wonders of lucid summary.) <br /><br />As a student of the novel, I say that Saragossa, still largely ignored by novel scholars, belongs on the same shelf with greatest half dozen novels ever written. This includes Don Quixote, which immerses its reader into just one culture, that of Cervantes' early 17th century Christian Spain. On this score, Potocki makes Cervantes look parochial. And he does so on stylistic grounds as well. Where Readers today must accommodate their ears to the formal rhythms of Cervantes' pure Castillian language, Saragossa, by contrast, and notwithstanding the extent to which it too immerses readers - plunges them headlong - into the Castillian Spain of Potocki's time. In so doing, it offers readers, as I said above, a panoramic view of the entire Mediterranean world, with each of its distinct 66 stories unified by the themes of heroism, love and nobility. <br /><br />These are Cervantic themes, and I think the case be made that their exposition in Saragossa is worthy of the creator of Don Quixote. Like Cervantes, Potocki maintains a serene detachment from any one culture or faith. And he does do, in part, by virtue of his dextrous and hugely innovative use of an ostensibly involuted novelistic form: that of the frame story: of stories within stories and (for Potocki) stories within stories within stories. So how, then, does Potocki manage to pull his readers along from one story to the next? The answer, I think, is that no story teller before or since has advanced his action in prose as graceful and steady as his. This straightforward, crystalline prose is as fresh to readers today as a piece in the New Yorker magazine. One can only marvel at its exquisite observational capacity, nothing short of cinematic. The man had a mind like a camera. (One of the quirks is that this book, in which Spanish culture is a primary concern, was written in French by a Pole. But fear not: Ian Maclean's tranlation in English, published by Penguin, has the lean, clean feel . . . of Spanish!). <br /><br />BTW, a fine companion book to Saragossa is Karen Armstrong's Battle for God, about the rise of fundamentalist strains of Christianity, Judaism and Islam that began (she argues) in Castillian Spain in the reign of Ferdinand and Isabella. A great, essential and supremely timely book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15367641956072911178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_586866594" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/586866594" />
    <a title="Tim Pendry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1016626-tim-pendry"><img alt="Tim Pendry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1210701989p2/1016626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/586866594?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim Pendry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim Pendry" href="/user/show/1016626-tim-pendry">Tim Pendry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780946626670" href="/book/show/93185.Tales_from_Saragossa_Manuscript">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=sexuality-erotica">sexuality-erotica</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=literature-general">literature-general</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=nineteenth-century">nineteenth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=modern-european">modern-european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=cultural-studies">cultural-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=heroic-fantasy">heroic-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=esoteric">esoteric</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=eighteenth-century">eighteenth-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1016626-tim-pendry?shelf=dark-fantasy">dark-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer586866594" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12754554089499326324"><br/>This is an overrated picaresque 'classic' from very early in the nineteenth century. It has its moments of genuine surprise and horror - indeed eroticism - but it is also overwrought, messy and confused.<br/><br/>Brian Stableford has produced a solid piece of academic background for this edition. We are really not very sure of the book's origin. Is it Polish or French and, if Polish, which Potocki wrote it?<br/><br/>There have been great cultural claims for this book - including claims of it holding secret qabbalis</span>
  <span id="freeText12754554089499326324" style="display:none"><br />This is an overrated picaresque 'classic' from very early in the nineteenth century. It has its moments of genuine surprise and horror - indeed eroticism - but it is also overwrought, messy and confused.<br /><br />Brian Stableford has produced a solid piece of academic background for this edition. We are really not very sure of the book's origin. Is it Polish or French and, if Polish, which Potocki wrote it?<br /><br />There have been great cultural claims for this book - including claims of it holding secret qabbalistic meaning - but it strikes me as the plaything of a bored aristocrat trying to work out his demons on paper.<br /><br />The most striking aspect of this quasi-Gothic tale is the underlying eroticism of what amount to mysterious and dream-like 'threesomes' that only get dignified with their qabbalistic coating towards the end.<br /><br />And, of course, we have our old nineteenth century neurotic friend - the linkage of sex and death. We find ourselves in the picaresque tale-telling world of one era and the decadent necro-sexuality of another.<br /><br />I am not sure about these claims at all. It seems to me that our mysterious nobleman was trying to cope with his sexual fantasies and then finding a way to give them erotic meaning through the esoteric.<br /><br />That may sound cynical until you consider how much libertine, homo-erotic and ephebophiliac sentiment was usefully hidden under neo-pagan cover right up until very recent times.<br /><br />In a Christian culture of aristocratic licence and religious reaction, the worlds of myth and the esoteric have both been tailor made for turning 'base' desires and urges into something 'magical' and 'other'.<br /><br />But the artistic output of sexual desire can be done well or badly. This book is so inconclusive that there is a danger of thinking its incompleteness hides some intended subtlety. I do not think it does.<br /><br />What might be said, though, is that 'Potocki' straddles the world of eighteenth century aristocratic licentiousness and what would become the bourgeois decadence of the era of Huysmans in a very peculiar way.<br /><br />The peculiarity of this is taken unwarrantedly for sophistication. What we have instead is an acute magpie mind, taking a number of literary influences and throwing them into what amounts to a dream novel.<br /><br />The tension between the honour codes of Alphonse Van Worden and the desire to be saved from eternal damnation is also a tension between fleshly pleasures and the desire for meaning.<br /><br />It could be said that the confusion and lack of completion in the book mirrors these tensions quite well but that is not enough. <br /><br />The 'manuscript' deserves its place in history (for study) but it is not a great book for all that.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12754554089499326324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1361284873" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1361284873?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/22026875-cheryl">Cheryl</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1361284873" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13815364054205348744">From the blurbs in the front of the book: "The 100 or so stories told over 66 days are fantastic, ghostly, erotic, comic, ghoulish, philosophical and Munchausenly tall." "One of the strangest books ever written can at last take its rightful place in world literature." <br/><br/>This has to be one of the most uniquely-written books I have read. Totally addictive, with continued inter-locking stories that are hard to put down. The book is plot-driven, but the characters within the stories are also very inte</span>
  <span id="freeText13815364054205348744" style="display:none">From the blurbs in the front of the book: "The 100 or so stories told over 66 days are fantastic, ghostly, erotic, comic, ghoulish, philosophical and Munchausenly tall." "One of the strangest books ever written can at last take its rightful place in world literature." <br /><br />This has to be one of the most uniquely-written books I have read. Totally addictive, with continued inter-locking stories that are hard to put down. The book is plot-driven, but the characters within the stories are also very interesting. The stories are pretty much over the top, but entertaining. The book is long (630 pages of smaller print), but I flew through it in 5 days. I give it 4 stars rather than 5 because two of the continued stories - of the Wandering Jew and of the mathematician - weren't really my favorites. (Too philosophical.) However, these can easily be skimmed through without missing anything vital to the overall plot. Some of the inter-connected stories could get a bit complex, but the author gives you enough reminders of how they fit so that you won't be lost. I'm really glad I read this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13815364054205348744" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_837397926" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/837397926" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549200708p2/5860412.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/837397926?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph">Joseph</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14769658273841684655">Count Jan Potocki’s <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i> has somewhat of a cult following amongst fans of Gothic fiction. It consists of a collection of supernatural tales linked together by a complex series of frame stories, as in a nightmarish hall of mirrors. It has been called a "black Decameron". This is a really apt description, considering that practically all Gothic tropes are represented in the convoluted text: from ghosts to vampires, secret societies to violent bandits, underground passa</span>
  <span id="freeText14769658273841684655" style="display:none">Count Jan Potocki’s <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i> has somewhat of a cult following amongst fans of Gothic fiction. It consists of a collection of supernatural tales linked together by a complex series of frame stories, as in a nightmarish hall of mirrors. It has been called a "black Decameron". This is a really apt description, considering that practically all Gothic tropes are represented in the convoluted text: from ghosts to vampires, secret societies to violent bandits, underground passages to haunted castles. A bonus for Melitensia enthusiasts – one of the stories features a Knight of Malta who murders a rival in Strait Street, Valletta just up the road from where I earn my daily bread (in decidedly more mundane environs).</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_445993211" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/445993211" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445993211?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13984676-lee-foust?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445993211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14375304019952764184">Although I feel as though this review will almost immediately drift into minor criticisms--provoked by my living in the hectic 21st century versus the leisurely pace of an 18th century in dealing with a text as episodic, long, as intricate, and as self-involved as <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i>--let me first say that this is a pretty fantastic novel, both for its engrossing tales, but primarily for those very intricacies and formal narrative somersaults that I have seldom found in other texts</span>
  <span id="freeText14375304019952764184" style="display:none">Although I feel as though this review will almost immediately drift into minor criticisms--provoked by my living in the hectic 21st century versus the leisurely pace of an 18th century in dealing with a text as episodic, long, as intricate, and as self-involved as <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i>--let me first say that this is a pretty fantastic novel, both for its engrossing tales, but primarily for those very intricacies and formal narrative somersaults that I have seldom found in other texts of this type. So what type is it? Good question. The translator's introduction calls <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i> a "novel in frames," which is a term I had not heard before but one which I will surely use a lot in the coming months as I, too, am writing one right now--I had been having trouble describing it to people too so this novel (and its introduction) has been indeed a propitious read for me right now.<br /><br />The novel-in-a-frame form, in this instance, entails a linear narrative--but a very thin, almost patina-like narrative--which digresses over and over again into tales told by characters who keep waking up under a gallows at a crossroads beneath the hanging corpses of a couple of thieves after nights of adventures or dreams, no one is quite sure which. There are even digressive tales within some of the tales. There are thieves, political intrigues, deaths and mystical journeys into hidden caverns and underground springs, lots of sexual threesomes, and one insanely shy maker of ink; in other words, a fantastic array of characters and adventures.<br /><br />The inevitable comparisons are to be made to Boccaccio's <i>Decameron</i> or <i>The 1,001 Nights</i> but I actually found the flavor of Potocki's novel to be more reminiscent of Gerard de Nerval's pseudo-mystical reveries or the granddaddy of all romance-style (that is to say episodic) novels-in-frames, (and, as I have argued in essays elsewhere on this very web, all Gothic romances as well) Apuleius' <i>Metamorphoses</i>, better known to English-speaking readers as <i>The Golden Ass</i>. <i>The Manuscript Found in Saragossa</i>, like Nerval's novels and semi-autobiographical extravaganzas, explores the mystical, the blurry edges of existence, and the cross-over borderlands between sleep and waking, familiarity and otherness, between Christianity and Islam (which makes Spain, for centuries at a time part of each religion, a perfect setting), sanity and madness, between self and other with a series of images and narratives about doubles, multiple love partners, and bands of outsiders. Reading such books is like taking a path into the woods that you know can only lead to your getting lost. It's usually worth it.<br /><br />Well, look, I managed not to write any of the banal criticisms that had worried me while reading--and it's better that way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14375304019952764184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_32777714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32777714" />
    <a title="Cailin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1517007-cailin"><img alt="Cailin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221091454p2/1517007.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32777714?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cailin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cailin" href="/user/show/1517007-cailin">Cailin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32777714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5570822456513309234">I read this for my senior seminar in college; the topic of the course was Possible Worlds. It was accompanied by a little too much Leibniz. Regardless, the sometimes exhausting tome was amusing, a little scary, and had a pleasing POINT... to those of us who love the labyrinth of literature and the idea of stories in life, as well as print. <br/>The characters, time period, and setting are all quite interesting; the plot too, is compelling. But what makes this book GOOD is the execution of something m</span>
  <span id="freeText5570822456513309234" style="display:none">I read this for my senior seminar in college; the topic of the course was Possible Worlds. It was accompanied by a little too much Leibniz. Regardless, the sometimes exhausting tome was amusing, a little scary, and had a pleasing POINT... to those of us who love the labyrinth of literature and the idea of stories in life, as well as print. <br />The characters, time period, and setting are all quite interesting; the plot too, is compelling. But what makes this book GOOD is the execution of something movies have been trying awfully hard to do lately: overlapping stories. The Manuscript is not just one story. It explores the possibility of a main story with a number of stories cropping up inside it - but it doesn't stop there. At one point, the stories at 19 levels deep. The main character will meet a traveler, who will tell him a story. Within the story he's telling, he'll come across another character, who will tell him his story, and within that story, another character will tell a story. <br />It sounds like more work that it is to keep track of where you are in the layers of plot, but since so many of the stories are in different places or time periods, it's mostly just entertaining. The real fun of the book, however, comes when you first realize that cropping out of all the layers of stories are very similar outcomes, or very similar characters. It becomes an almost Pynchon-like maze of conspiracy, which our protagonist attempts to ignore, despite his unease.<br />This novel would be much more likable it if made it's point in 300 pages instead of 700, but it is interesting, and plot is unique enough to carry the reader through, nonetheless. If statements like "there are only two endings to a story: either they live happily ever after, or they die," and "there are only so many stories, and they've all been told; we're just finding new ways to tell them," then you'll have fun with the philosophy behind this paperweight.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5570822456513309234" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10915448259946101965">I've never tried hallucinogenic drugs, and I'm not sure I'll ever have to so long as a copy of this book is readily at hand. It's hard to pick a favorite scene -- a strong candidate is the one where a 16-year-old Jewess kabbalist-in-training kills a she-bear with a book cover (made of wood from the Ark of the Covenant, natch) and then fights off its cubs to suck on the dead bear's swollen teats for sustenance. Moments later the bear comes back to life, sort of, because it's possessed by a maybe-</span>
  <span id="freeText10915448259946101965" style="display:none">I've never tried hallucinogenic drugs, and I'm not sure I'll ever have to so long as a copy of this book is readily at hand. It's hard to pick a favorite scene -- a strong candidate is the one where a 16-year-old Jewess kabbalist-in-training kills a she-bear with a book cover (made of wood from the Ark of the Covenant, natch) and then fights off its cubs to suck on the dead bear's swollen teats for sustenance. Moments later the bear comes back to life, sort of, because it's possessed by a maybe-evil demon.<br /><br />But really, almost any scene will do. This book is many things -- Bildungsroman, picaresque, Wunderkammer, Enlightenment philosophical treatise, Grand Guignol horror tale, acid trip -- and I'm not sure it all hangs together on a macro-level, but scene by scene, it is never less than riotously entertaining.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_18866557" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18866557" />
    <a title="Ned" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/318782-ned"><img alt="Ned" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1209975199p2/318782.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18866557?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ned" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ned" href="/user/show/318782-ned">Ned</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670834280" href="/book/show/2917174-the-manuscript-found-in-saragossa">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/318782-ned?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18866557" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5574767009927922847">this is my third read of this in ten years. A rarity, that.<br/>Timeless. Always enigmatic. These are overused today as hyperbolic advertisement for things like cheese-puffs and belly-button rings. This book isn't like that at all.<br/>Maybe I should tell the story about how I found this one.<br/><br/>Before the internet, before I got a second computer I used to keep lists. On paper with a pen lists of news topics. Alphabetized for later much easier access I would make my lists til there was no more room on the pag</span>
  <span id="freeText5574767009927922847" style="display:none">this is my third read of this in ten years. A rarity, that.<br />Timeless. Always enigmatic. These are overused today as hyperbolic advertisement for things like cheese-puffs and belly-button rings. This book isn't like that at all.<br />Maybe I should tell the story about how I found this one.<br /><br />Before the internet, before I got a second computer I used to keep lists. On paper with a pen lists of news topics. Alphabetized for later much easier access I would make my lists til there was no more room on the page of so I had to make a new list leaving plenty of spaces between each entry so that over time each space could be full of new info and so on. Several books of those lists still exist and would be useful for the periods I did them in finding newsworthy topics as covered by the newyorktimes. Buggers make you pay for these articles now and all the ones I kept I believe are at the bottom of some trash heap somewhere, so the record-keeping, the newspaper keeping, all of it was a big waste of time. Except it taught me some very good 'stuff-management' skills that I shan't forget whenever I might go travelling in a far off distant country.<br />That being said, one of the things I kept in the lists were new book publications that would show up in the newyorktimes book review that comes out every week. I would read the section avidly every week and books or reviews I liked would get notated by author, title or sometimes subject or location, depending how zealous I was in keeping my lists that week. But this title and this author kept finding their way to the tops of my alphabetized lists. For like six months. So on one of the few expenditures for entertainment that year (sometimes that's just how it is) I basically chose this book at random from my lists.<br />Well it turned out to be a very different kind of book. It's a creepy religion conversion attempt set in a world full of shifters, 250 years ago. But most of the bulk of the book are stories told one after the other. People's histories as they remember them are spread out over successive days of wandering from site to site with a troupe of gypsy's. Most of the stories are told by the leader of this group, more or less. He shows himself to be a complete scoundrel, but everyone asks for him to continue his stories and as they branch off, as he introduces new characters in his narrative and supplies a bit of backstory for them, he launches off in a whole direction and these people and their relatives always end up leading to the plot shift of the last story which in turn the other characters will pick up on in their own way for their character's story. There's a supraterrestrial destined to walk the earth for aeons, there are a brother and sister pair of magi the one looking for the twin daughters of solomon and the sister looking for a way out, a mathematician, some other travellers, herders, priests, our Muslim heiresses and our protagonist. He gets lost in these mountains in the mid 1700's in a southern Spain that can still just quite remember her arab heritage and feels the battles between a dying, superstitious church and the winds of Romantic Rationalism. The wanderers are all depicted as individual types of free-thinker out in the no man's land of that day's borderland, in a time fraught with cracks all over in any supposed system of law or order. After a century of wars with England, Spain was mostly ruled by the French Bourbon line and was no longer (in the period of our story) such a great power. The book purports to be a translated copy of a work found in Spanish in a trunk in Spain. But the Saragossa described is not the actual Saragossa that's in Spain. This takes place far to the south in this wild overgrown region<br />But why would the author tell this kind of story? Jan Potocki was a Polish aristocrat living in the height of Euro- Napoleonic-mania and the years after where everything seemed to need a hero. Why indeed. It can be read as a kind-of romantic-gothic coming of age story, a Spanish set of Arabian Nights told in 66 days. The people in the stories aren't ever very heroic, but are often cruel. It seems there are several sets of revenge stories and dead people condemned to work out their frustrations on the living. All of them lost, all of them well aware of their ssurroundings just not what to do. Or if they know what to do the surroundings change and more is revealed and so things have changed. And then another story or new piece of info on this other story is revealed until you become lost in it and it no longer matters which story goes with which character or set of stories. <br />It seems to be an unfinished manuscript.<br />The author may have died several different ways.<br />He had several different careers, several different wives, and contested paternity estate suits after he died, but he died in debt.<br /><br />You can read this book randomly, like the Aeneid used to be read in the Middle Ages.<br />"Just pick it up and read a little of it to me, dear. My eyesight's not so good, see, and I would just like to hear the sound of some of those beautiful words... yes, anywhere is fine. Just start anywhere."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5574767009927922847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
